Hong Zhaolin
§YBorn:§! §LApril 3rd, 1876 in Ningxiang County, Hunan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LIndependent§!\n§YBackground:§! §LSon of a poor family, Hong Zhaolin would join the Yongzi Battalion of the Guangdong Army at the age of 20, becoming a captain just before the Xinhai Revolution. Under instigation of Chen Jiongming and Deng Keng, he would join the revolutionaries in Huizhou. In 1912 he was appointed as the head of the Guangdong Army Mixed Corps and the Director of Huizhou Pacification. Fighting in the Second Revolution, he would ultimately be forced into exile in Japan.\n\nReturning to Huizhou in 1914, he plotted to overthrow Long Jiguang, but after failing he would flee to Hong Kong just to be arrested by the local authorities. Released by revolutionaries, he would again return to Huizhou in 1916 to overthrow Long, this time succeeding and being appointed as Assistan Director of Huizhou by the new Governor, Zhu Qinglan. Serving in the Guangdong-Fujian Army, he would fight against Li Houji in Fujian, and in the First Yue-Gui War under Chen Jiongming, becoming the local warlord of Chaomei.\n\nMaintaining parity with Chen Jiongming after June 16th, Hong would dupe the KMT, align with Cao Kun and the Zhili Clique, and then welcome Chen Jiongming back in 1923. Appointed Chaomei Protector by Beijing, Hong would fight in the Eastern Expedition, and bitterly hold onto the Chaoshan area. Seriously wounded in battle, Hong would be forced into retreat to recuperate. Briefly solicited by Sun Chuanfang to join him in his army, Hong would be dispatched by Sun to Guangdong during the scramble for Liangguang in 1927, rallying forces in Chaoshan, acting as an awkward ally of Shen Hongying under the same master, with ties to Ye Ju.§!\n\n§YNotes: He is notorious for running a rampant opium smuggling ring under the auspices of Chen Jiongming in the 1920s, and is on the outer rung of Haifeng and Yue politics.§!
Liu Zhenhuan
At a young age Liu Zhenhuan was surrounded by rich family and many friends. Enjoying a joyful childhood, he would join the Tongmenghui in 1911, and carry out revolutionary activities all the way until Xinhai. Immediately dispatching bandits such as Shen Hongying to aid the revolution, Liu organised his local militia, and in 1912 become an official commander within the Guangxi Army. Advocating immediate support of the Second Revolution, Liu would find himself in Hong Kong, and then Shanghai on behalf of the Kuomintang.\n\nReturning to Guangxi in 1916 to aid in the National Protection War, he fought against Long Jiguang in 1918, and aided the Guangdong Army in 1921 to fight the Old Guangxi Clique. Aiding Tang Jiyao in his return to Yunnan, Liu would ultimately side with Sun Yat-sen in the Second Yue-Gui War, and again during Shen Hongying's revolt.\n\nDissatisfied with the Reorganisation of the Kuomintang, Liu began to abuse his position, and his already lax discipline in his soldiers caused much outrage. In 1925 he endeavoured with Tang Jiyao to oust the New Guangxi Clique and Sun Yat-sen, but after its failure he formed an alliance with Yang Ximin, Deng Benying, Chen Jiongming, Duan Qirui and others and rebelled against the KMT in 1925, ultimately resulting in his own deposition. Lamenting in exile in Hong Kong, Liu would be hired by his old friend Chen Lianbo in 1927 to control the western approaches to Guangzhou.

Li Fulin

Yang Ximin

Lin Zhengxuan

Aiding with negotiations with various Beiyang officials, and of securing at least tacit calm with Shen Hongying and Lin Hu, Lin has found himself indespensible within Guangdong's politics.§!\n\n§YNotes: Together with Yang Ximin and Liu Zhenhuan, the trio form the resident provincial branch of PSC Confederates, and much like Liu, is amenable to Third Party democrats.§!
Huang Dawei
§YBorn:§! §L1886, in Huangpi County, Hubei Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LIndependent§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the Royal Military Academy in Belgium, and the Meiji University in Japan, Huang Dawei joined the Tongmenghui during his time in Europe, and returned in 1911 to fight in the Xinhai Revolution. Serving as a military officer of the Provisional Presidential Mansion in Nanjing, he was promoted to Colonel of the Army Artillery and Major General in 1912. In 1917 he served as an officer of the Generalissimo's Office during the Constitutional Protection Government, and in 1920 served as commander of the first route of the Guangdong Army.\n\nIn 1922 he was serving in the aborted Northern Expedition, but after Chen Jiongming's June 16th Incident, he led his troops to retreat to Fujian with Xu Chongzhi, and became the local warlord of Chaoshan and Commander-in-Chief of the forces on the Fujian-Guangdong border. Later that year he served as commander of the first army under Xu Chongzhi, and in 1923 was promoted by the Beiyang General's Office.\n\nIn early 1923 Huang defected from the Pro-Sun camp to Chen Jiongming, and served as commander of Chen Jiongming's 7th Army, and director of Chaoshan Border Defence. From July 1923 to February 1924 he, alongside Lai Shihuang, Liu Zhilu, Lin Hu, and Wang Xianchen, established the United Forces Office Government in Zhangzhou, Fujian. Collapsing in 1924 due to defection and Pro-Sun advances, he would flee to Hong Kong with the rest of Chen Jiongming's allies. Returning in 1927 under Chen Lianbo's employment, he jockeys with Hong Zhaolin for control of the Chaoshan region.§!\n\n§YNotes: Noted for arrogance and disobeying orders due to ego.§!
Yang Chisheng
§YBorn:§! §L1891, in Shiping County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LIndependent§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the cavalry department of the Yunnan Military Academy, and a close friend of Yang Ruxuan, Yang Chisheng would serve in the Double Ninth Uprising, and the National Protection War. Fighting under Cai E, he would be promoted in 1917 to regimental commander in the Huguojun, and later brigade commander. Supporting Gu Pinzhen's anti-Tang coup, he would be ejected from Yunnan and end up as a component of the Yunnan Army in Guangdong. Working with Yang Ximin and Yang Ruxuan to exclude Zhang Kairu from power, he would elect Ximin as Commander-in-Chief of the Yunnan Army in Guangdong.\n\nFighting against Chen Jiongming on behalf of the Kuomintang, he would ultimately be removed from his position in 1925 as divisional commander, and expelled from Guangdong due to collaboration with Wu Peifu and the Zhili Clique. Collaborating in-part due to Yang Ximin's manoeuvres, he would then fully defect and serve as commander of the 1st Division of the Yunnan Army under Wu Peifu, and garrison commander of southern Jiangxi.\n\nDuring the Northern Expedition he would fight against the 4th, 7th and 14th Armies of the National Revolutionary Army in Jiangxi, successfully triumphing as the National Revolutionary Army collapsed. Shuffling into Guangdong in pursuit of the retreating NRA, Yang would end up within Shen Hongying's sphere of control, as a warlord of his own calibre with Yang Ruxuan. With a tense relationship with Shen himself, Yang maintains distance with the Shen Army faction, forming their own sub-clique with Yang Ruxuan, stuck between a rock and a hard place.§!
Yang Ruxuan

Graduating in 1913, he would participate in the preparations for the National Protection War, and fought under Cai E's first army in the sixth regiment under Zhu De. Fighting bravely and fiercely, he would be seriously injured, and promoted for his merits.\n\nParticipating in Gu Pinzhen's coup attempt in 1920, he would fight the bandits in Southern Yunnan, effectively wiping them out. Following Sun Yat-sen's request, the Yunnan Army exiles travelled from Guizhou to Guangdong to fight against Chen Jiongming, and subsequently against the Guangxi Army led by Shen Hongying. Appointed as commander of the Second Division of the Yunnan Army in Guangdong, with Chisheng as commander of the First Division, he would be slighted by Yang Ximin, and begin communications with Wu Peifu.\n\nOuted by Yang Ximin as potential defectors to Wu Peifu, Ruxuan and Chisheng would be dismissed by Sun Yat-sen, and then subsequently defect to Wu properly as commanders of the Yunnan Army under the Zhili. Fighting against the National Revolutionary Army, he would be contacted by Zhu Peide to defect, but ultimately refuse follwing Kuomintang failures on the battlefield. Folding into Guangdong in pursuit of the Kuomintang, he would form a sub-clique with Yang Chisheng within Shen Hongying's area of control.§!\n\n§YNotes: He is sympathetic to the revolutionaries.§!

Tang Tingguang
§YBorn:§! §L1865, in Huaxian County, Guangdong Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from Huangpu Naval Academy, Tang Tingguang was sent to study the navy in the United Kingdom. Returning to China, he would fight in the Sino-Japanese War in 1894 as captain of the Dingyuan Ship. Appointed captain of the Haiqi warship in 1909, he accompanied Cheng Biguang to serve as representatives to the coronation of George V of England, and later toured the White House and met President Taft.\n\nAwarded with a medal by Yuan Shikai in 1913, he would follow Cheng Biguang and other navy officers to the Constitutional Protection Government in 1917, and was appointed by Sun Yat-sen to serve as a military officer in the Generalissimo's Office, and then as Deputy Minister of the Navy. Fighting the Guangxi Clique in 1919, he would become Governor of Guangdong in the interim chaos and handed the position over to the Guangdong Army. Being rewarded as Minister of the Navy of the new Government.\n\nDuring the June 16th Incident, he wrote an open letter with Wei Bangping and Ye Ju demanding that the fighting cease, but his subordinates resolutely supported Sun Yat-sen, and he resigned in September. Appointed by Chen Xiru back into office, he would remain when Sun Yat-sen returned in 1923. From 1925 onwards he resigned and worked in his local county, before being recommissioned by Chen Lianbo to serve as Minister of the Navy within his Provincial Government.§!

Ding Ying
§YBorn:§! §LNovember 25th, 1888 in Gaozhou City, Guangdong Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWith his father believing the poor were often abused by the gentry because they were uneducated, Ding Ying would thus be sent to private schooling, the first in his family. Excelling in school, he was exposed to revolutionary ideals in his institution, and actively joined student groups. Intending to study agriculture to improve the lives of farmers, missing an exam period resulted in his application to Guangdong Normal University's museology department.\n\nStudying in Japan in 1912, he learnt Japanese, and participated in Chinese student-led public protests in 1919. Returning that same year, he taught at local schools, but returned to Japan in 1921 to study at the Tokyo Imperial University. Graduating with a Bachelor's in Agriculture, he would teach in the Guangdong Public Agricultural School, later National Sun Yat-sen University, College of Agriculture. Establishing experimental rice cultivation farms, he bred a total of 110 rice varieties, and in 1934 achieved the world's first hybrid rice variety with wild rice.\n\nUnder Ding's efforts, Guangdong has become the rice breeding centre of Southern China, and he is considered something of a local icon and celebrity.§!\n\n§YNotes: Lives a particularly simple and frugal life, and lives a life as selfless as it is philosophical.§!

MMIC - Liangguang Branch
§YMission Militaire Internationale en Chine's Liangguang Branch§!\n§YBackground:§! §LDeployed to China by the Third Internationale following the League War, the MMIC is the evolution of prior socialist efforts to aid the Chinese revolution. Initially consisting of Red Russian émigrés fleeing their civil war sent to aid Dr. Sun in 1924, it has since drawn officers from across the socialist world in an international effort befitting of its sponsors.\n\nThe current head of the Mission is Frenchman André Malraux, who serves a dual role of advising the National Revolutionary Army and promoting it back home. Other figures include David Ramsay of Britain, Manabendra Nath (M.N.) Roy of India, Jean Fontenoy of France, Ingvald Berentin Aase of Norway, and Jakob Rudnik (Paul Ruegg) of Ukraine.\n\nA mixture of political and military officers, their motivations are mixed. Some are drawn by the excitement of an adventure on the other side of the world, others by the tantalising prospect of fame and advancement they could not otherwise achieve at home. Some simply wish to fulfil a sense of internationalist obligation, while others have an affinity for Orientalism.\n\nMany younger NRA officers, particularly those trained abroad or sympathetic to syndicalism, are quite fond of their MMIC trainers. However, they have drawn criticism for their willingness to engage in Chinese politics - seen as undue meddling of internal affairs by many in the Old Guard. The MMIC on the other hand, believe themselves to be the necessary counterweight to reactionary influences in the NRA high command.§!
German Military Mission
§YBackground:§! §LRunning off of the background of the Sino-German Military Mission of the 1920s and 1930s, there has been an increasing cast of Pro-German cadets and officers educated in academies within Europe who have provided an ample landing ground for a further reigniting of Sino-German cooperation. Whilst the original Sino-German Military Mission found itself implemented sparingly in regions outside of the League of Eight Provinces, there has undoubtedly been hard lessons learned between the limits of which the Germans can exhert themselves on Chinese military-political spheres.\n\nLargely confining themselves to tacit pressure in politics, the rejuvenated Sino-German Military Mission focuses mostly on the sphere of war, bringing with them the attitudes of Germany's leading military groups; total war, mechanisation and mobility, or refinement of tried and true methods from the last World War. Either way, the renewed presence of German Officers in Chinese Headquarters may be a sign of a new dawn for Chinese Warfare.§!

Guangdong University
With the revival of the educational system within Guangdong that suffered under the Warlord period, the Guangdong University now serves as one of the frontiers of the patriotic Chinese Intelligentsia. Increasing the studies of technology that will help China not only in terms the future of the people but also in it's defence.
Guangzhou Hankou Railway
The new Guangzhou Hankou Railway company focuses on modernising the ways of transport on areas that we would originally had to rely on rivers on delivering supplies across, especially in Hankou which was a key example of this. However with the GHR, their help will make getting to these places like Hankou much easier.

Currency Instability
Our economic experts have recommended that we swiftly employ a programme of devaluation of the Liangguang Yuan in order to fight back against economic collapse, however such action has produce numerous bouts of ire and contention from the gentry and merchants, yet the broad farming peasantry supports our efforts, seeing their personal situations improve.
Economic Aid to Failing Industries
In order to save our local domestic industries and prevent even further unemployment within Liangguang, we have sought out local business leaders and merchants, offering them benevolent monetary aid in return for their continued functioning and support.
Pared Military Spending
Whilst our own independent military once shined as a beacon of provincial independence in the light of Zhili meddling, it has transformed into a bloated, inefficient mess. Withdrawing considerable funding in such critical times will hurt deeply, but sacrifices have to be made.
Struggling Literacy
By the turn of the 20th century, China's literacy rate reached a significant disparity with the rest of the world despite the formation and spread of modern education as a result of growing ties with the rest of the world. Irregardless, as a region, Guangdong finds itself as one of the most literate centers of China with some reports recording that the literacy rate in metropolitan areas might be close to 80-90%.\n\nNonetheless, rural areas in Liangguang such as Guangxi continue to struggle with widespread illiteracy and it will take some serious reforms in education in order to administer widespread provincial literacy.
Unfinished Business
Liangguang has never fully recovered from the political instability of the 1910s-1920s. A revolving door of governments took hold over Guangdong and Guangxi as warlords, revolutionaries, and others took turns seizing power from one another. The defeat of the Kuomintang's National Revolutionary government in Guangzhou by the Zhili backed by German support allowed for Old Guangxi Clique warlords to take power, but their externally aided rule has been shaky. Guangdong warlords have rallied around Guangzhou, with the diminished but not fully extinguished Federalist movement enduring. Meanwhile, Kuomintang and other revolutionary cells continue to operate across the two provinces, hoping one day to return to power.
German Style Army
Inspired by the works of Bauer and Seekt on their purposals of reforming the military; focusing on a smaller yet more mobile forces, set under a centralised command that is only loyal to the state with each assignee set to duties that relate to their expertese. This also allows resources to be under the control of the state to serve the army during times of war.
Japanese Style Army
Japan, though poses a threat to China, is a perfect model to reflect our military on, concidering they're the first in Asia to beat back an European power through full force of spirit, using the might of the infantry and skills of their airforce to both follow the code of Bushido to overcome the imperialistic Europeans.
Anglo-American Style Army
The Americans and their English kin are admired for a reason, focusing on tactics using artillery and making stronger use of the industry to supply the armies with an endless supply of equipment and not only to mention their commitment to be loyal to civilian governments, by mimicking them, we will be able to overcome our many foes.
Divided Regional Military
Liangguang is after all two provinces and with such a unique region naturally comes the problems of regionalism due to Guangxi and Guangdong's regional, cultural, and linguistic differences that have pervaded into the military forces of the province. The Guangdong Army is divided into a multitude of regional cliques and has fallen obsolete in comparison with forces such as the Fengtian Clique in the North while the Guangxi forces have been compared to be nothing more than organised bandit clans. As long as these forces remain divided and inadequate, Guangdong's potential to bid for a chance at national relevancy are but merely a fool's dream.

The Whampoa Spirit
Renowned for their sheer ferocity in the face of overwhelming odds, the cadres of Whampoa were like no other soldiers during the Northern Expedition, only being defeated by overwhelming technological and numerical advantage. With the reopening of Whampoa military academy, and the revival of its culture, a new generation of politically conscious, relentless and resilient soldier will be produced, for the ultimate unification and advancement of the Chinese nation.

The Eliteheer Model
Following the natural conclusion of German attempts to professionalise the Northern and Southern Zhili armies of Wu Peifu and Sun Chuanfang respectively, we have sought to no longer be considered mere auxilleries to their elite forces, but to implement our own programme of a German styled military. With an elite core surrounded by lesser support forces, the emulation of the Victors of the Weltkrieg is sure to bring great successes to our government.

Local Officer Initiative
To ever maintain our advantage and cunning on the battlefield, we have delegated the tactical initiative to the officers actually foreseeing the combat, as opposed to some lofty general far from the frontlines. With this we hope to craft a culture of independent initiative in our individual units in conjuncture with the whole army, ultimately to reach the same mutual goal.
The Guangxi Model
Espoused by renowned Guangxi natives such as Bai Chongxi and Ma Ji, the hardy, bandit-filled, mountaineous landscape of Guangxi has bred a series of ingenious tactis to survive in such a region. Total mobilisation of the people, running parallel with a mindset and initiative aiming to circumvent the expectations of our opponents, our army is that of true Citizen-Soldiers.
The Guangxi Model
$GXC_guangxi_army_idea_desc$
The Guangxi Model
$GXC_guangxi_army_idea_desc$
The Threefold Self Reliance Policy
Taking the critical Three Principles espoused by Dr. Sun Yat-sen: Democracy, Welfare, Nationalism, the Guangxi model formulates the Three Principles of Self Reliance: self-government, self-sufficiency, self-defence. China must be united utilising Chinese arms, material and manpower only, and in such, the nation must also be reconstructed by the same men who fight for it.
Youth Paramilitarism
Unfortunately for a place as torn asunder like China, the youth's livelihood has been completely annihilated by the likes of perfidious warlords and bandits. In order to defend the nation from warlords and imperialists alike, the young boys and girls will be taught the skills necessary for the nation's survival from a young age, so that future generations shall never experience the humiliation that we have.
Rousing the People
In order to revive the spirit of our great nation, it is necessary that we unite all classes of the people in a common struggle for national defence, in preparation for a great bloodletting. Only out of this great bloodletting can our nation secure its existence and independence from foreign powers!
Creating Modern Reserves
The very precipice of our nation's survival could depend on its ability to muster sufficient reserves to reinforce our divisions in times of great strife, therefore in order for China to rise against foreign powers around the world we will implement a modern reserve system modelled on the contemporary military powers of the world.
Unified Militia Framework
We cannot simply rely upon imitating the old provincial militia system, rife with infractions against the common people, corruption, abuses of power, and above all else seemingly lacking in effectiveness and capability. Instead the Guangxi System should aim for a unified organisational framework, with extensive regulation and ideological motivation. Only then shall we truly reach the new horizons of modern warfare.
Chinese Warfare, Revitalised
Our militia system is not a new invention, nay in fact it is a way of waging warfare endemic to the very heart of the Chinese people. The dynasties of old all practised our system, we have merely reinvigorated it, bringing it to the new age of warfare. Our soldiers and citizenry are one in the same, like a sentient organism. May the unity in our peoples strike far and wide against our enemies!
Political Commissars
Inspired by the combat squads of the SZRS and the political commissars that make up the Russian Army as well as the former socialist influence on Whampoa, the resurrected National Revolutionary Army seeks to uphold the commissar model. Non-commissioned officers are instructed to lead their men in battle and in livelihood, demonstrating exceptional worth and commitment to the cause of national revolution as well as providing lectures on the infallible doctrine of the Three Principles of the People. If China is indeed to be saved, then the education of the masses must begin with the armed forces - so that they will be truly an army dedicated to the cause of revolution.
Dare to Die Corps
Salvation does not come to those who wait but rather to those who are willing to give it all, their livelihoods, their families, and most of all their lives for the freedom and liberty of China. As the bugle sounds and the revolutionary soldier rises to take his stand in this pivotal moment of Chinese history, bold and courageous individuals should seek to form dare to die corps in the mold of the martyrs of the Xinhai Revolution, so that they too can offer their lives in sacrifice for a free China.
Widespread Workers' Unrest
Since the start of Year of the Rat, a wave of working class unrest has spread rapidly through the city of Guangzhou - an unprecedented number of workers and seamen have been taking to the streets to clamour for reform since the failed 1932 Shanghai Uprising. Guangzhou's position by the sea and its existing urban networks have already been aroused several times with notable examples being the Canton-Hong Kong Strike of 1925 and the recent 1932 Uprising.\n\nThe League of Chinese Syndicalists and the Kuomintang are no doubt behind this recent wave of unrest that threatens to envelop and disrupt the province. Each day, the tones of the protestors continue to grow more and more belligerent and only time will tell whether or not the workers decide to take up arms on the barricades.

The modern Chinese Federalist rose and fell across the first half of the 1920s. A coalition of constitutionalists, democrats, and regionalists - they banded together amid the continued North-South stalemate at the peak of the warlord era. Their calls for the various provinces to work towards implementing local self-government and then peacefully unite together into a federal republic achieved some traction, but after a promising start it was rejected by both northern and southern governments. The Zhili's successful unification by force discredited the movement, and many came to associate the movement as little more than a front for warlordism.\n\nRebuilding the momentum that was lost over a decade ago will be difficult, but the with the country once again seemingly hopelessly divided and the Zhili clique's liberalising efforts proving to be a sham, many hold out hope that the Federalist movement could be the solution to China's problems.

Lasting Legacy of the Cohong
The Cohong was the name granted to the Chinese merchant guild that solicited and operated the infamous import-export monopoly in Canton during the Qing dynasty, and for a time was the only point of access for Europeans within Chinese markets. Receiving immense profits and a practical monopoly on foreign trade by the Qing court, the Cohong would see a rapid expansion of finances within Canton, turning it into a true Pearl of the East. However, with the end of the First Opium War, the Cohong was dismantled, albeit this was not the end of the dominance of Cantonese Merchants.\n\nTransitioning from the Confucian bureaucratic merchant-to-merchant model of the Qing, the Cohong's successors would transform themselves into that of the British Empire's \

Empowered National Legislature

Constitution of the Republic of China
Forming the Fundamental Law by which the Republic governs, the Constitution of the Republic of China serves to be the final step on the long road to true constitutional rule within the Chinese nation. Drafted by the Political Consultative Council bringing together some of China's greatest legal minds from within the Kuomintang, namely the Reconstruction Faction of Sun Fo, the Western Hills Clique of Ju Zheng, the Elders Clique of Hu Hanmin, and even some minor participants such as the Political Science Clique of Huang Fu, it is a testament to the Kuomintang's duty, servitude, and diligence in China's state building project.\n\nHighlighting the Three Principles of the People as the guiding principles upon which the nation should be administered, and the concept of sovereignty of the people, the constitution also clearly stipualtes the protection of the people's freedoms and civil rights, the central government's own separation of powers into five governmental organs - the Five Yuans, and the local autonomy system.\n\nOutshining the previous attempts at constitutional rule within the Chinese nation, such as the Cao Kun Constitution, or the Temple of Heaven Draft Constitution, it is an inherently progressive piece of legislature. Composed of three parts, the Constitution of Liberty, the Constitution of Government, and the Constitution of Sovereignty, it retains 175 articles in total divided into 14 chapters.

A New Worker's Utopia

In Pursuit of the Federalist Ideal
The first iteration of the Federalist movement sought a plethora of lofty goals for the nation to fulfill: abolition of provincial and local military governments, demobilisation of the autonomous armies of the provinces, and the establishment of autonomous regional governments under civil control. It is under these slew of demands that various Federal Constitutional projects around the nation emerged, attempt to wrest control from military strongmen and the corrupt national government, and restore it to provincial civilian rule.\n\nUtilising grassroots civic mobilisation, the Federalist movement was ultimately nourished by the failures of central government efforts to both unify and centralise the nation. Bearing no fruit, ideological theorists such as Zhang Shizhao and Zhang Taiyan emerged to cultivate the concept of a Federalist Ideal. Whilst the further interpretation of their ideas ranged far and wide, the ideal remained largely the same: the sovereignty of the people belongs in the establishment of autonomous provinces operating according to their own constitution.\n\nIt is this ideal that we strive for, and hell hath no fury, neither domineering centralist demagogues nor idling militarists shall rip apart the movement ever again. Uniting Liberals, Socialists, Constitutionalists all under one umbrella, and through the good will of the National Army, Federalism shall see its spotlight yet.

Multi-Partisan Chinese Democracy
The testament to the ultimate pursuit of idealism from the Chinese Southwest Federalist movement is that of its innovative brand of Multi-Partisan Democracy. Building upon the grassroots mobilisation of intellectuals from the 1920s and 30s, the Federalist movement thrived and was nourished due to the lack of stability in the wake of the Zhili collapse. The National Army Commission
As advocated by members of the cabinet and political arena, a National Army Commission has been instituted nationwide as an entity under the Ministry of Internal Affairs. With the ultimate aim of inter-party collaboration and coordination, the commission serves to reduce the size of the armed forces to a mere national defence level from the ballooned size of the Warlord Era, and as a result lessening the financial burden on the state. It also serves a dual functionality: finally nationalising the military, and vetting all appointments so as to ensure loyalty to the incumbent government.
Ministry of National Security
With the further extension of the state of Martial Law under Marshal-President Ye Ju, the government has deemed it fit to transform the National Army Commission into a Ministry of the government itself. Tasked with the maintenance of all Public Security bureaus within the nation, the National Security Bureau works closely with the military to ensure dissent is squashed as swiftly as possible, and that the maintenance of harmony and order perseveres throughout the nation.

The reality is much more nuanced. Many warlords were well-educated and kept up to date with international political trends, and while most had a ruthless streak, that was hardly uncommon in China. Relentless Military Regionalism
As a result of the inherent nature of the unification wars and the extensive reliance upon warlord forces, alongside the relentless promotion of regional autonomy rights, the nascent Chinese Republican Government has become saddled with a catastrophic issue of their own making. The warlords, both new and old, have begun to corner off their domains of territory from the intrigues of the Central Government, substituting what should have been national unification with a mere facade of unity on the surface. Ignoring Central Government directives, tax payments, and relentlessly opposing initiatives to centralise the provinces or otherwise the military, the Warlords have seemingly become beholden to one indomnitable fact: if they are to keep their wealth, power, and influence, then they very well might have to take up arms against the new government itself.
Deteriorating Financial Sector
Due to decades of corruption, economic imperialism from foreign powers, unstability due to national states of crisis, disunity, and civil war, alongside a plethora of other factors, the Chinese economy is, suffice to say, in a rather poor state. Investors have shirked potential opportunities, hyperinflation has resulted in a worthless currency, bandits continue to raid any remaining domestic commerce or agrarian activities. Ever present issues continue to raise their head, such as the consistent dilemma of land reform, and cross-provincial economic integration; with local communities even pressing for immediate economic relief, compromising with the nation's recent unification. What is for certain, is that the nation necessitates stable economic management, and national reconstruction.
The Anti-Democratic Cordon Sanitaire
Despite claims of fighting for the republican values and virtues of the Xinhai Revolution, the advancements in national democracy have been anything but as comparable to the lofty ideals of China's various democrats. Plenty of positions, from the Minister to the Magistrate have been granted not too those most meritorious, or those elected, but those within \
Reaffirming Professionalism
What was once a motley crew of disparate warlord forces, now a bloated, ineffective, and highly protective \
Zenith of Chinese Capital
With direct access to the executive office, the Chinese Merchant and Business class have truly asserted themselves at the height of their role within the nation. Now an integral function of Chinese reconstruction, the endeavouring Chinese capitalists have become one of the most adhered to voices within the nation. Overseeing bounding economic progress and ever increasings profits after decades of war and bloodshed, the businessmen of China are architects of the nation's renewal, financiers of its military consolidation, and negotiators of its financial independence.
Rule of the Iron Willed
There is no room in government for those lacking in the qualities of chivalry, charisma, or decisiveness. Only those of iron wills, those who have fought either in the battlefields or the high offices are worthy of being entrusted with national leadership. To steer China onto a new dawn requires conviction, courage, and bravery. And these virtues are exemplified in the halls of the new Central Government. Whilst issues of dissent, disagreement, or corruption may make its way to the news outlets, the overwhelming presence of a national front of bureaucrats and technocrats dissuades any advancing perceptions of supposed weakness.
Pursuing New Policy
Under the initiative of Zhu Jiahua, the Beiyang Republic has begun to increasingly import European political scientists, researchers, professors, and businessmen, with the ultimate aim of implement the President's ambitious \
Order and Progress
Little politics, much administration. The role of the Central Government is not to become a slave to ideology, or to personalist strongmen, or even to intellectuals, monarchs, or scholars. Ensuring economic progress, no matter the price paid, will ensure that China is forever a strong nation. No longer shall the Chinese bask in a Century of Humiliation. Pursuing the policy of a Carrot and Stick, the people will be ensured happy lives and fulfilling careers, as long as they toe the line with the incumbent government.
The Gentleman's Republic
Ruled by personal agreement, handshake, or banquet, the new Chinese Republic is much akin to that of the old; politics ruled by personalities, not ideology. Individuals matter more than the perceived policy plan of a political architect, and as such national policy is highly favoured towards variety, pragmatism, and the implementation of necessary measures to ensure continued progress on the fronts of national reconstruction, economic development, and assertation of China's position on the world stage.
The Washingtonian Republic
So-called the \
The Monrovian Republic
Promulgating an era of peace, stability, and political harmony, the Political Science Clique's own focus on Anti-Partisan democracy has led to a broad united front, led by Political Science, and joined by fellow third parties across the board. Having long sought to oust the dominance of authoritarian warlords from the avenues of executive power, their victory against the Merchants has brought forward a period of national calm, for the first time in decades. Always seeking a broad consensus on policy, the new Chinese Republic has begun a rapprochment with various political parties, and a policy of restoring dignity to the political office, once admonished.
A New Republic
Led by some of its most prominent fighters in the early days of the Chinese Republic, the new Beiyang Republic has brought forward its own unique interpretation on the annals of the Five Races Under One Union, and the legacy of the Xinhai Revolution. Seeking to revitalise the Republican movement after the ailing effects of decades of warlordism and corruption, the Young Marshal of the South and his Young Guangxi allies bring a new generation of politicians to the Chinese national office. Individuals who scarcely remember the Qing, or otherwise rebelled against it. Onlooking onto new horizons however, many of the Young Marshal's old allies with ties to the Zhili Clique advise caution, or apprehension, at pursuing total progress.
Ode to Xinhai
There could be no Chinese Republic without the brave sacrifice of its fighters in the Tongmenghui and the Xinhai Revolution, and it would be but a tarnishing of their memory to institute a new sense of autocracy or authoritarianism upon their democratic aspirations. Whilst the nation cannot truly exercise the Four Civil Rights necessary for successful democratic governance, the Central Government can nonetheless work towards the careful cultivation and advancement of the lives of its citizens on the hope, that in the future, however far that may be, China will be able to truly implement the nascent dream of 1911.
A Moral Republic
To pursue total progress at any and all cost is a foolish game; China is a nation with thousands of years of civilisation, and it is undoubtedly unwise to cast aside any and all advancements of previous eras of Chinese civilisation for the folly of democratic governance. Democracy is the will of the people, and separation of powers is the principle of such democracy. Serving as the foundation of the nation's politics, the passing of power to the people is but a certainty. However, in the interim it is crucial that the nation be ruled by Good Men, experts in their fields, akin to the scholars of old. Only by looking towards tradition, can the nation begin to move forward.
Studying European Political Science
As was the original intentions of the Political Science Clique's predecessor, the European Affairs Research Society, and as is the modus-operandi of the Association of All Classes to Save Guangdong and its Cantonese-British style democratic management, it is abundantly necessary for the Chinese democratic republic to be modelled upon that of the successful examples in the West. Whilst democracy has seen dire straits in recent times, various nations still exercise and respect the will of the people, consent of the popular masses, and that of representative political power through elected officials. If China is to ever be a truly democratic nation, it must but only examine, critique, and refine the methods of the existing international democratic states. Already, various officials have been sent to various countries to analyse their methods, and conclude on their most fitting applications in the Chinese context.
Twin Provinces, One Destiny
The two provinces that make up Liangguang, Guangdong and Guangxi, are very different but nevertheless share a common history and a common fate. As the new year approaches, tensions continue to rise as Kuomintang and Federalist agitators threaten to unravel the delicate system Lu Rongting has built. No one knows what will happen next, but what is assured is that it will happen together.
Initiate Economic Stabilisation
As the Year of the Rat dawned, many hope the year would bring prosperity and stability but the cascading impact of the global economic meltdown has caused severe a downturn in China. While Guangdong's industrial sectors are bruised, Guangxi's rural economy has been devastated by falling exports and domestic overproduction. We must move quickly to slow the economic free fall.

Devalue the Liangguang Yuan
To maintain our regional independence and in defiance of central government efforts, Liangguang has long had a de facto currency of its own, which has quickly depreciated in value. Vested financial interests have warned against playing games with its value, however more enterprising \
Bail Out Failing Industries
Across the last decade, various Liangguang economic programs worked to industrialise the region, with varying effect. The heart of this effort has been in Guangzhou, and many companies (both private and government-run) are on the verge of shuttering their doors. A concentrated effort to save them and reassure business leaders must be made, lest we lose our industrial advantage over our rivals.
Pare Military Spending
As part of Lu Rongting's efforts to protect against Sun Chuanfang's encroachments and also bring other Yue-Gui warlords to heel, Liangguang has become heavily militarised. In his dogged pursuit of military parity with Sun, the army has grown severely bloated and inefficient. Much of the money flows into the pockets of the various generals and bandit leaders to keep them loyal. They will have to make sacrifices in these dire times.
Lessons of the German Advisory Mission
The German Advisory Mission, deployed to support the victorious Zhili clique in maintaining control of their post-1926 conquests, has paid little heed to various provincial forces, treating them as colonial sepoys meant to soak enemy bullets for the central army. Still, our proximity to multiple key legations has afforded us a little more attention than others as they seek to protect their investments.
Consolidate the Regional Auxiliaries
While it would be unfair to say all German advisors have treated Chinese forces as disposable, the object of their limited affections have been the elite central army divisions of Sun Chuanfang and Wu Peifu. Even then, an oft-repeated criticism of the post-Restoration era military reforms is the Chinese army has become effectively an \
Insistence Upon the Stiff Arm
After the end of Fourth Zhili-Fengtian war, an attempt at reforming provincial police forces was made. The Cantonese police took inspiration from American and German police models, taking a national lead in modernising police work. Continuing these reforms, new police academies must be created and efforts made to reinforce a positive public image of incorruptibility, civil order, and public peace.
Blockhouse Tactics
Many German advisors drew inspiration not only from their experiences fighting in the Weltkrieg, but also earlier colonial conflicts in Africa. To root out ongoing insurgencies in the League, a series of encirclement campaigns began following the 1932 Shanghai Uprising. Fortifying key strong points across Liangguang denies enemies easy movement, allowing mobile elements to isolate and destroy them.
Never Another Guangzhou
After the collapse of the British Empire and the breakdown of the Tsingtao Accord, the \
From Flanders' Fields to Guangxi's Mountains
Perhaps the only constant about warfare is that it is always changing. With another period of bloodshed on the horizon, we must work to integrate the lessons of the past few decades drawing from experiences in the Weltkrieg, the National and Constitutional Protection Wars, the Northern Expedition, and the Zhili-Fengtian wars. Difficult times lie ahead, but there is no sense marching blindly.
Adopt Battlefield Radios
One of the greatest innovations of the 20th Century is the mass improvement of communication technologies. This has allowed for Generals to command increasingly large formations and has had a proven history of tilting the scales on the battlefield. Whether it be fighting conventional battles or dealing with rural insurgencies, radios will give us the edge we need to prevail.
Integrate Provincial Security Forces
Liangguang has long been home to a menagerie of bandit clans, rogue detachments, and warlord militias vying for influence. Regional governments have often only asserted minimal control over these petty strongmen, preferring instead to unleash them against other governments or pit them against each other. Having faced the worst crisis yet, we must now work to assert our authority over Liangguang.
Primacy of the Southern Piedmont
The quality of Chinese armies varies tremendously. Some units are hardly one step above bandits, while others only exist on paper. Others, particularly those funded by the central government or the personal retainers of stronger warlords are more elite, though hardly up to par with western armies. In some ways this is by design, allowing an almost feudal-like security structure. But just as China must change if it is to survive, so too must our armies.
Integrate the Baojia
Local military detachments are often haphazardly organised, equipped with whatever weapons that can be easily fashioned by local power-holders and staffed by men levied from the fields and hamlets. These militias have often been overlooked in the eyes of our sneering Western trainers, but we know that if properly integrated these brave men protecting their homes can be an asset in asymmetrical conflicts.
The Necessary Sin
In order to finance our armies, we have been forced to export raw resources in bulk. In particular, Liangguang has made good use trading cheap Wolfram (Tungsten) in exchange for more advanced military equipment from western powers, allowing us to keep Zhili influence at bay. Continuing this trade is essential to funding our elite troops, even if one day we hope to negotiate on a more equal footing.
Tighten the Noose on Militarism
The decentralised, clan-like nature of warlordism has often been a source of frustration of foreign observers. Officers come and go, taking their entire staff along with men and arms like feudal times. The Germans are not too dissimilar, packing their bags and running once times grew tough. We however, remain. We shall begin asserting civilian control over the army, starting with little things like having civilian police check army officers' bags for contraband.
Embrace the Guangxi Style
For all of Guangxi's flaws, its people have developed a proud military culture that, if properly harnessed might save the nation. From exile, Bai Chongxi has written at length about his theories, most prominently calling for \
The Literati and Officer Corps Are One
Just as every able-bodied man must serve as a citizen-soldier, every student must be willing to become an officer. Bai and other military men have long castigated the student class for being pacifistic, status-hungry, and anti-militarism despite trying times, looking down on military service while whining about foreign imperialism. The new Chinese student will be aggressive, patriotic, and self-sacrificing.
Age of Scientific Warfare
A new era of warfare dawns on the world, and China must not fall behind in the art of war. Wars and war-making grows exponentially complex, and literacy among the officer corps becomes increasingly a necessity. Only civilian schools can provide literate officers in time and in sufficient quantities, so students and intellectuals must be pressed into national service. Through mind and muscle we will prevail.
The Militia Cadre Academy
Quality officers make the difference between citizen-militia and ordinary rabble. While initially decentralised training at cadre-training battalions placed in each militia region seemed promising, we have found it to be more efficient to have one central academy in Guangxi to give cadets two months of training. After that they will be shipped off to regional militia Headquarters for further training. Better officers will later become field commanders or staff officers.
Encapsulating the Warrior Society
In building a citizen-army, we must implement a training programme radically different from the disorganised warlords and the corrupt professional armies that came before us. Instead, a comprehensive programme beginning at age 17 covering both military and non-military matters will prepare young men for the difficult realities that lie ahead. Across eighteen meticulously planned months, they become a new breed of soldiers, and more provinces shall follow our example.
Merge Army and Militia
