Heir to the legacy of the Guangzhou Government, Marshal Lu Yuguang finds himself at the crossroads of fate. Although his Old Guangxi Clique is master of Guangxi and Guangdong, the adopted son of Lu Rongting finds his regime pressed from all sides. From the East, Federalist and disgruntled Cantonese elements seek to end a decade of Guangxi dominance. From the West, the Kuomintang stands ready to restore Nationalist control over the South. Will the Young Marshal of the South weather the coming storm, or will he find himself consumed by the tides of Revolution and Federalism?
GFX_GXC_intro
§RThe Hotbed of Revolution§!
Long the champion of anti-Northerner influence within the nation, the Pearl River Delta's history in the Warlord Era is marred by revolt, struggle, banditry, and revolution. With a looming anti-Qing struggle occurring within the region since the end of the §YTaiping Heavenly Rebellion§!, the Pearl of the South is naturally home to many of China's present day great revolutionaries. Forming revolutionary groups such as the §lTongmenghui§! and other likeminded revolutionaries, Liangguang (referring to the joint region of §YGuangxi§! and §YGuangdong§!) had its own precursor uprisings before §lXinhai§! such as the §lSecond Guangzhou Uprising§!. These groups finally played their part in the §lXinhai Revolution§! as a republican stronghold. With the seizure of Guangdong by §lChen Jiongming§! and §aHu Hanmin§!, and §YGuangxi§! by §PLu Rongting§!, the region would remain steadfast in the hands of the Republicans. With the declaration of the §YSecond Revolution§!, and its ultimate failure, §aHu Hanmin§! was ousted as Governor, and replaced by §lChen§!, only for him to be ousted similarly and replaced by §PYuan§! Loyalist §PLong Jiguang§!.\n\nWith the onslaught of the §lNational Protection War§!, §YGuangxi§! tepidly announced their support after the §aHuguojun§! saw initial outstanding successes, and marched into §YGuangdong§! to depose the §PYuan§! loyalists. After a brief interim Southern Government hosted by National Protection leaders such as §SLiang Qichao§!, §mLi Genyuan§!, §aCen Chunxuan§!, and others, the §POld Guangxi Clique§! effectively united §YLiangguang§! under their dominion, at least on paper. With the chaos of the Warlord Era now in full swing, §eBeiyang§! infighting would see the proclamation of the §aConstitutional Protection Government§! in 1917, with its capital in §YGuangzhou§!. Led by the legendary §aSun Yat-sen§!, it included the likes of §lChen Jiongming§!, §cXu Chongzhi§!, §aTang Jiyao§!, §PLu Rongting§!, §lTang Shaoyi§!, §aHu Hanmin§!, §cLi Liejun§!, and numerous others. Eventually being hijacked and ousting §aSun§!, the §aConstitutional Protection Government§! attempted peace talks with the North.\n\nHaving been driven into §YFujian§!, §lChen Jiongming§! and §cXu Chongzhi§! initiated the §lFirst Yue-Gui War§! in 1920, successfully ousting the §POld Guangxi clique§!, and instating §lChen§! as governor of §YGuangdong§!. With §aSun§! insisting upon a new extraordinary government that would grant him executive powers, a political schism with §lChen§! formed, and after fighting the §lSecond Yue-Gui War§! in 1921, which sent §YGuangxi§! into practical anarchy, the two clashed over the §YNorthern Expedition§!, Unification by Negotiation or by Subjugation, and Federalism over Centralism. Tensions ultimately culminated in the §YJune 16th Incident§!, with the attempted shelling of §aSun's§! Palace by §aYe Ju§! and the §aOld Yue Clique§!.\n\nEngulfed in consistent conflict, many individuals would clash, and then defect or otherwise. With the rise in influence of warlords such as §PDeng Benyin§!, §mLiu Zhenhuan§!, §aYang Ximin§!, §PShen Hongying§!, §aLin Hu§!, §PHong Zhaolin§!, and numerous others, §YGuangdong§! effectively became a patchwork of influences. With the region inflamed further by the §YMerchant Corps Incident§! of 1924, wherein §YGuangzhou's§! capitalists rose up to protect themselves in fear of §kKMT§! atrocities, the §PWhampoa Training Corps§! and §PAcademy§! were formed that same year, and would soon drive out both §lChen Jiongming§! and §mChen Lianbo's§! forces. No longer willing to endure alliances with warlords in order to see unification of the nation, §aSun Yat-sen§! initiated the Reorganisation of the Kuomintang. Meanwhile, in exile, §lChen Jiongming§! founded the §lPublic Interest Party§! in 1925.
§lParty-State Infighting and the Northern Expedition§!
Politically, the §kKuomintang§! transformed radically. As reformists and Federalists flocked to the various Federalist leaders in the nation, the Party was reorganised in 1924, establishing a democratic centralist form of organisation, an alliance with the §RInternational§!, and a United Front with the §rChinese Syndicalist Party§!. With §aSun§! heading north to engage in peace talks, the Party fractured further due to lack of his leadership in the South. After his death, acting Generalissimo §aHu Hanmin§!, §aSun's§! presumed successor, engaged in a power struggle with §RWang Jingwei§!, §PChiang Kai-shek§!, §RLiao Zhongkai§!, §cZhu Peide§!, and §cXu Chongzhi§!. Simultaneously, §aTang Jiyao§! attempted an invasion of §YLiangguang§!, only to be halted by recent §kKuomintang allies§!, the §PNew Guangxi Clique§!.\n\nWith many rightist party members moving to the §YShanghai§! Headquarters due to increasing §Rleftist§! activity in §YGuangdong§!, §aHu§! was ousted by a §YNational Government Council§!. While he had almost managed to clinch victory from §RWang§! and §PChiang's§! hands, the assassination attempt on §RLiao Zhongkai§! caused a mass purge of rightists within the Party by §aHu's§! enemies. Reinvigorated, the United Front would hold strong, and the schism between the §aOld Right§! and §RLeft§! wings of the party grew larger. Growing disillusioned, the §aOld Right§! held the §cWestern Hills Conference§! in late 1925, and were subsequently expelled by the §kCentral Executive Committee§!, later fleeing to §YShanghai§!. With the political situation somewhat stabilised in favour of the United Front, the §kKuomintang§! launched their §YNorthern Expedition§! from §YLiangguang§! under the stewardship of §RWang§! and §aChiang§! in July 1926.\n\nThe expedition was led with the newly formed §PNational Revolutionary Army§!, consisting of the reorganised and committed allied warlord armies alongside the newly elite §PWhampoa-born 1st Army§!, led by §PChiang§! and §PHe Yingqin§!. Alongside the §P1st§!, was §cTan Yankai's 2nd§!, §cZhu Peide's 3rd§!, §lLi Jishen's 4th§!, §aLi Fulin's 5th§!, §aCheng Qian's 6th§!, §PLi Zongren's 7th§!, and §rTang Shengzhi's 8th§!. Initially successful, reaching §YWuchang§! in September, the §YNorthern Expedition§! was soon crippled by the German Intervention in October, overrunning §YGuangzhou§! by November. While the §k4th Army§! attempted reasonable resistance, the §a5th§! disintegrated, causing §PChiang§! to reorient the §P1st Army§! south in an attempt to relieve the siege. Seen as abandoning the front, this move was incredibly unpopular, and their intense assault resulted in failure.\n\nWith §YGuangzhou§! in German hands, their relatively high casualties resulted in their refusal to move further, instead opting for their §zZhili§! allies to finish the job; appointing §mChen Lianbo§! as Mayor in the interim. §PChiang's§! failure saw his ousting by §RWang§! with the support of §lLi Jishen§!, §lZhang Fakui§!, §RTang Shengzhi§!, §cTan Yankai§! and §cZhu Peide§!. Meeting in private with §PHe Yingqin§! and §PBai Chongxi§!, §PChiang§! resigned and was replaced by §lLi Jishen§!. With the §P1st§! no longer responding to orders and only directly listening to §PChiang§!, §RDeng Yanda§! and the §Rleft-wing§! militias organised a strike in §YGuangzhou§!. In January 1927 the §YBattle of Jinhua§! was fought by the §l4th army§! with assistance from the §c3rd§! and §R8th§!, causing the practical disintegration of the §l4th§! and §R8th§! armies as they retreated to start a guerrilla struggle in §kMinGan§!.\n\nReturning to power due to §lLi's§! failure, §PChiang§! swiftly initiated a political marriage with §aSong Meiling§!, intimidated §PHe Yingqin§! back into cowed alliance, and with fear of the left's influence was backed by §cZhu Peide§! and a (reluctant) §PLi Zongren§!, alongside an incredibly apprehensive §aCheng Qian§!, to the position of Generalissimo. While Chiang ordered the §YLong March§! westward to §YGuangxi§!, §YGuizhou§!, and southwestern §YHunan§!, §RWang Jingwei§! and §RLiao Zhongkai§! would lead the §RCentral Executive Committee§! into exile.\n\nWith §nYuan Zuming's§! defection to §eBeiyang§! and an attempted invasion yet again from §aTang Jiyao§! prior, the §YLong March§! turned disorderly, and following §PChiang's§! assassination in early February, would result in a drove of defections, and the assumption of control of the §PNRA§! by §cZhu Peide§!. Now tepidly based in §YGuizhou§! (due to the §aYunnan Clique's§! own infighting preventing their ousting) and parts of §YGuangxi§!, the §kKuomintang§! was slowly pushed deeper into the former, and the §YNorthern Expedition§! officially came to an end in February 1927 with the Party-State continuing its struggle in §YGuizhou§!, §kMinGan§!, and §YShanghai§!.
§gThe Liangguang Interregnum§!
Leaving chaos in their departure, the advancing §zNorthern§! and §MSouthern Zhili§! armies began to use irregulars and auxiliary formations from local factions to further shore up control as §eFengtian§! initiated the §Y3rd Zhili-Fengtian War§! in February 1927. Instigated by clashes between §PZhang Zongchang§! and §cWu Peifu§!, the §zZhili§! would deftly deal with §PZhang Zuolin§! and the §eFengtian Clique§!. In the meantime, the §POld Guangxi§! and §aOld Guangdong Cliques§!, with members having either fled to §zZhili§! lines or retired, returned to power and influence within their localities. With the §MSouthern Zhili§! rather ambiguously settling on §PShen Hongying§! as Governor of §YGuangdong§!, §mChen Lianbo§! retained his German appointed and §zZhili§! affirmed Mayorship of §YGuangzhou§!, in-part due to negotiations required for the German withdrawal. Neither would control the majority of the province, and through the acquisition of former warlords on §mChen§! and §PShen's§! payrolls, Guangdong further fractured into fiefdoms.\n\nIn §YGuangxi§!, the various disparate clans competed for unification in the void left by §PNew Guangxi's§! retreat, ultimately resulting in §PLu Rongting§! managing to manoeuvre himself back into power, to the surprise of many. With his venerable age and reputation shoring out any issues of control, he was reluctantly recognised as Governor of §YGuangxi§! by the §zNorthern Zhili§!. To the south, in §YQiongya§!, §aDeng Benyin§! returned to power in the island after a concerted effort from §mChen Lianbo§! and the Germans, establishing a tepid alliance between §YQiongya§! and §YGuangzhou§!.\n\nSlowly expanding his sphere of control, §mChen Lianbo§! himself begun hiring former §aGuangdong Army§! (both §kKuomintang§! and Federalist) officers to further bulk up his §mMerchant Corps§!, resulting in the employment of individuals such as §aChen Jitang§!, §lChen Jiongming§! (albeit he refused a military position), §aYe Ju§! and others. In conjunction with this, he hired individuals such as §mLiu Zhenhuan§!, and §aYang Ximin§!. With the conclusion of the §Y4th Zhili-Fengtian War§! and the §YCER Conflict§!, the §zZhili§! announced scheduled elections, and in the meantime §PSun Chuanfang§! formally incorporated Hunan, §YGuangxi§!, and §YGuangdong§! into the §YLeague of Five Provinces§! in 1929, becoming the §YLeague of Eight Provinces§!.\n\nWith the new elections of 1929 resulting in a §aHarmony Association§! landslide, the §lUnited National Construction League§! was formed by democratically minded Chinese intellectuals from the §nYoung China Party§!, §cDemocratic Constitutionalist Party§!, §mPolitical Science Clique§! (recently legalised), §lPublic Interest Party§!, §cPeople's Government Party§!, and plenty others, although the inclusion of the likes of §kKuomintang§! adjacents such as §RZhang Bojun§! and §RTan Pingshan§! drew heavy scrutiny. With §PZhang Zuolin's§! refusal to entertain unification negotiations after the §zZhili§! landslide election, §PSun Chuanfang§! triggered the §YLeague Crisis§! of 1930 as he ambiguously responded to overtures from §PZhang Zuolin§! about restarting the Civil War.\n\nPromoting Federalist rhetoric to protect the §YLeague's§! autonomy, amongst other power plays, his actions would see the §YShanghai Uprising§! erupt in 1932. Resulting in catastrophic reverberations throughout the nation, it saw the fleeing of intellectuals into the interior, and the resulting interior policy from the §YLeague§! itself saw §PShen Hongying's§! backers retreat, crippling his position. Swiftly §PLu Rongting§! coordinated an alliance with §mChen Lianbo§! and §aDeng Benyin§!, pushing §PShen§! out of major positions in §YGuangxi§! and §YGuangdong§!. In return the §zNorthern Zhili§! recognised §PLu§! as §YViceroy of Liangguang§! to check §PSun Chuanfang§!. Similarly §PLu§! signed an under-the-table agreement of mutual defence with §cZhao Hengti§!; should §PSun§! attempt to re-assert himself within the Southwestern provinces.\n\nAs 1934 dawned, §PLu Rongting§! died of natural causes, leaving much of his work to centralise the provinces of §YLiangguang§! uncomplete. His adopted sons, §aLu Yuguang§! (his groomed successor, and the Young Marshal of the South), and §aMa Ji§! took his place. Rearing their §aYoung Guangxi§! faction of the §POld Guangxi Clique§! to the forefront, they form a triumvirate, even quadrumvirate, between §aLu§!, §aMa§!, §aLiu Zhilu§!, and §aLin Hu§!. §PShen§! remains, hardly holding on, whilst §lChen§! continues his nominally \
Political Diaspora in Yue-Gui
§MThe Guangxi and Guangdong Cliques§!\nIn the chaos and anarchy of the §kKuomintang§! collapse many parties and factions, both old and new, have returned or otherwise appeared at the forefront of §YLiangguang§! politics. Chiefly amongst them, the §POld Guangxi Clique§! and its disparate internal factions, the Federalists within the nation, gentry and merchants, and all other manner of individuals. As de-facto the most powerful group within the region, the §POld Guangxi Clique§! remains disunited, split between the §aYoung Guangxi Faction§! and the §PShen Army Faction§!, with minor clans in and amongst their ranks.\n\nThe §aYoung Guangxi Faction§! profess a broad stroke ideological tendency towards old-style Republican Revolutionary politics, with ties to the §mPolitical Science Clique§!, and various other political groups. Ultimately quite pragmatic, they remain focused on extending ther influence within the South of the nation. Their chief rivals are the §MSouthern Zhili§! backed §PShen Army Faction§!, based around the eponymous §PShen Hongying§!. Ailing and elderly, the bandit turned statesman has long been yearning to stake his claim as Governor of §YGuangdong§! even in the diminishing circumstances. With a tepid alliance with the §aJingxing Society§! and other Pro-German Pro-South Zhili interest groups in the region, his disparate band of bandits seek to confirm their power promised to them in 1927.\n\nOperating within the urban hub of §YGuangzhou§! are those keen on wheeling and dealing, with a firm handshake masquerading less than noble intentions. With Mayor §mChen Lianbo§! exercising authority through his §lAmerican-inspired§! §mCanton Merchant Corps§! and extensive business connections within the province, he has managed to hire and invite numerous intellectuals and independents to his flock. Primarily represented through Senator §lChen Jiongming§!, founder and leader of the §lPublic Interest Party§! (founded in 1925), championing Federalism and democracy in the region. On his flank is the §SNational Renaissance Society§! led by Zhang Junmai, formed in 1932, as a subsect of the §cChinese Constitutionalist Party§!. Focused on adapting German Social Democracy to China, it carries a notable socialist tint with members such as §RZhang Dongsun§! championing Guild Socialism.\n\nSimilarly, they are joined by independent members of the political battleground, such as §mLin Zhengxuan§!, Speaker of the Assembly and member of the §mPolitical Science Clique§!. Other individuals associated are the likes of §aLin Hu§!, §mLiu Zhenhuan§!, and §aYang Ximin§!. Similarly the gentry are represented through the local §cGrain Industry Maintenance Association§!, having long been backers and friends of §lChen Jiongming§!, they have renewed their strength to protect their interests. In addition local warlords such as §aYang Ruxuan§! and §aChisheng§!, alongside §aDeng Benyin§! amongst others, scatter to protect their interests. Hardly a democratic cornucopia, §YGuangzhou§! nonetheless remains a Pearl on the River Delta.\n\n§MThe Kuomintang§!\nWhilst the §kKuomintang§! have very little overt influence within the region, they still retain sizeable unnofficial influence. Skirmishing militias in §YGuangxi's§! east, §rPeng Pai's§! rural movement in §YGuangdong's§! peasant slums, and various §kMinGan§! irregulars flowing in and out across the border co-exist with the influence of \

§L\
Dongjiang River Column
The Dongjiang River Column (sometimes translated The East River Column), is a band of a socialist anti-imperialist guerillas that sought to resist foreign occupation. Organised as a stay behind force, they have been trained in blending in with the local population and sabotaging efforts by the enemy to consolidate their hold on the valuable Southern Chinese trade network. Although their numbers vary, their efforts have been mythologised in official government propaganda.
Qiongya Autonomy
Receiving an influential position within the government, Deng Benyin has been able to almost fully carve off the island of Qiongya as his own fiefdom, near enough to asking for provincial status, Deng has instead opted to maintain parity with the government. As long as he has an ear within the upper chambers of power, he is content to support provincial and national ambitions.
The Five Year Plan
Under the ambitious plans of the Democratic Socialists, the whole Frontier of China and the regions north of the Great Wall have been earmarked for continuous and concerted economic development. To transform vast swathes of desert, mountain, and steppe into economically productive land is no small undertaking, but already the various regions have seen remarkable economic improvement.
Free Enterprise Encouraged
By allocating sections of various industrial zones within the nation for foreign investment and industrial development, the government has propelled a serious of \
Entrenched Yue Dominance
With further \
Special Economic Zone
On the road to strengthening the Chinese Republic, the nation can only achieve true independence and assert itself on the international stage if it can achieve a certain level of economic development independent of that of foreign enterprises. As nations abandon laissez-faire economic policies on the gradual move towards socialism, China itself must still maintain zones of capitalist economic development. Under these, it is hoped that their economic prosperity will spread all throughout the nation.

Balancing the Levers of Government
Harmonious balance is a virtue that all Chinese governments must aspire towards, and the current iteration of the Beiyang Republic is no different. Imposed by Old Guangxi aficionados, various necessary alliances and old friends have been brought together under the notion of continued administration, and the pursuit of a national strengthening campaign. The Central Government has become split into three faces: the Political Science Clique, the Jingxing Society, and the various compatriots of the Old Guangxi and Zhili Cliques. Together, they ward off any attempts from outside influences to intercede on their governance.
